<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Logos_and_uniforms">Logos and uniforms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Logos and uniforms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Logos_and_uniforms_of_the_Kansas_City_Chiefs" title="Logos and uniforms of the Kansas City Chiefs">Logos and uniforms of the Kansas City Chiefs</a></div>
<p>When the Texans began playing in 1960, the team's logo consisted of the state of <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a> in white with a yellow star marking the location of the city of Dallas. Originally, Hunt chose <a href="/wiki/Columbia_blue" title="Columbia blue">Columbia blue</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orange_(colour)" title="Orange (colour)">orange</a> for the Texans' uniforms, but <a href="/wiki/Bud_Adams" title="Bud Adams">Bud Adams</a> chose Columbia blue and scarlet for his <a href="/wiki/Houston_Oilers" class="mw-redirect" title="Houston Oilers">Houston Oilers</a> franchise.<sup id="cite_ref-Uniform_History_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uniform_History-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Hunt reverted to red and gold for the Texans' uniforms, which even after the team relocated to Kansas City, remain as the franchise's colors to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-Uniform_History_2-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uniform_History-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The state of Texas on the team's helmet was replaced by an <a href="/wiki/Arrowhead" title="Arrowhead">arrowhead</a> design originally sketched by Lamar Hunt on a napkin.<sup id="cite_ref-Uniform_History_2-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uniform_History-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Hunt's inspiration for the interlocking "KC" design was the "SF" inside of an oval on the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_49ers" title="San Francisco 49ers">San Francisco 49ers</a> helmets.<sup id="cite_ref-Uniform_History_2-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uniform_History-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Unlike the 49ers' logo, Kansas City's overlapping initials appear inside a white arrowhead instead of an oval and are surrounded by a thin black outline.<sup id="cite_ref-Uniform_History_2-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uniform_History-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> From 1960 to 1973, the Chiefs had grey facemask bars on their helmets, but changed to white facemasks in 1974, making them one of the first teams (alongside the San Diego Chargers, who introduced a yellow facemask that same year) in the NFL to use a non-gray facemask.<sup id="cite_ref-Uniform_History_2-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uniform_History-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chiefs' uniform design has essentially remained the same throughout the club's history.<sup id="cite_ref-Uniform_History_2-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uniform_History-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It consists of a red helmet, and either red or white jerseys with the opposite color numbers and names.<sup id="cite_ref-Uniform_History_2-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uniform_History-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> White pants were used with both jerseys from 1960 to 1967 and 1989 to 1999.<sup id="cite_ref-Uniform_History_2-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Uniform_History-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Beginning in 2009, during the Pioli/Haley era, the team has alternated between white and red pants for road games during the season. Prior to September 15, 2013, the Chiefs always wore white pants with their red jerseys. The Chiefs have never worn an alternate jersey in a game, although custom jerseys are sold for retail.
</p><p>The Chiefs wore their white jerseys with white pants at home for the 2006 season opener against the Cincinnati Bengals. The logic behind the uniform selection that day was that the Bengals would be forced to wear their black uniforms on a day that forecasted for steamy temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The only other time the Chiefs wore white at home was throughout the <a href="/wiki/1980_Kansas_City_Chiefs_season" title="1980 Kansas City Chiefs season">1980 season</a> under <a href="/wiki/Marv_Levy" title="Marv Levy">Marv Levy</a>.
</p><p>In 2007, the Kansas City Chiefs honored Lamar Hunt and the AFL with a special patch.<sup id="cite_ref-patch_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patch-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> It features the AFL's logo from the 1960s with Hunt's "LH" initials inside the football.<sup id="cite_ref-patch_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patch-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> In 2008, the patch became permanently affixed to the left chest of both Kansas City's home and away jerseys.<sup id="cite_ref-patch_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-patch-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In select games for the 2009 season, the Chiefs, as well as the other founding teams of the <a href="/wiki/American_Football_League" title="American Football League">American Football League</a>, wore "throwback" uniforms to celebrate the AFL's 50th anniversary.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs_to_unveil_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs_to_unveil-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the first time in team history, the Chiefs wore their red jersey with red pants forming an all red combo in their home opener against the Dallas Cowboys on September 15, 2013. Their all red uniforms are now an official uniform combination and has been used multiple times since. It is commonly used for prime time games at home. The all-red look also served as the basis for the Chiefs' <a href="/wiki/NFL_Color_Rush" title="NFL Color Rush">Color Rush</a> design.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arrowhead_Stadium">Arrowhead Stadium</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Arrowhead Stadium">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Arrowhead_Stadium" title="Arrowhead Stadium">Arrowhead Stadium</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Arrowhead_Stadium_2010.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Arrowhead_Stadium_2010.JPG/220px-Arrowhead_Stadium_2010.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="93" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Arrowhead_Stadium_2010.JPG/330px-Arrowhead_Stadium_2010.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Arrowhead_Stadium_2010.JPG/440px-Arrowhead_Stadium_2010.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3280" data-file-height="1384" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Arrowhead_Stadium_2010.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Arrowhead_Stadium" title="Arrowhead Stadium">Arrowhead Stadium</a> upon completion of renovations, July 2010</div></div></div>
<p>Arrowhead Stadium has been the Chiefs' home field since 1972 and has a capacity of 76,416,<sup id="cite_ref-seating_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seating-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> which makes it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_current_National_Football_League_stadiums" title="List of current National Football League stadiums">fifth-largest stadium</a> in the NFL. The stadium underwent a $375 million renovation, completed in mid-2010, which included new luxury boxes, wider concourses and enhanced amenities.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pioli_to_join_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pioli_to_join-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> The stadium renovation was paid for by $250 million in taxpayer money and $125 million from the Hunt Family.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs_hire_Cardinals_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs_hire_Cardinals-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The stadium cost $53 million to build in 1972, and an average ticket in 2009 costs $81.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aramark" title="Aramark">Aramark</a> serves as the stadium's concession provider and <a href="/wiki/Sprint_Corporation" title="Sprint Corporation">Sprint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anheuser-Busch" title="Anheuser-Busch">Anheuser-Busch</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> are major corporate sponsors.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Dating back to the Chiefs' home opener in 1991 to mid-2009, the Chiefs had 155 consecutive sellout games.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The streak ended with the final home game of the 2009 season against the Cleveland Browns, resulting in the first local TV blackout in over 19 years.<sup id="cite_ref-NFL&#39;s_best_fans_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NFL&#39;s_best_fans-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Arrowhead has been called one of the world's finest stadiums<sup id="cite_ref-PFHOF_8-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PFHOF-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and has long held a reputation for being one of the toughest and loudest outdoor stadiums for opposing players to play in.<sup id="cite_ref-Pioli_to_join_60-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pioli_to_join-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Peter_King_Notes_–_The_NFL_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_King_Notes_–_The_NFL-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> All noise is directly attributed to its fans<sup id="cite_ref-NFL&#39;s_Top_10_Fan_Bases_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NFL&#39;s_Top_10_Fan_Bases-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> and was once measured at 116 <a href="/wiki/Decibel" title="Decibel">decibels</a> by the Acoustical Design Group of <a href="/wiki/Mission,_Kansas" title="Mission, Kansas">Mission, Kansas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs_ready_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs_ready-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> By way of comparison, take-off of aircraft may lead to a sound level of 106 decibels at the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs_ready_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs_ready-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Sports_Illustrated" title="Sports Illustrated">Sports Illustrated</a></i> named Arrowhead Stadium the "toughest place to play" for opposing teams in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Tailgate_party" title="Tailgate party">tailgate party</a> environment outside the stadium on gameday has been compared to a "college football" atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Arrowhead Stadium features frequent <a href="/wiki/Flypast#Entertainment_and_sports" title="Flypast">fly-overs</a> from a <a href="/wiki/B-2_Spirit" class="mw-redirect" title="B-2 Spirit">B-2 Spirit</a> <a href="/wiki/Stealth_bomber" class="mw-redirect" title="Stealth bomber">stealth bomber</a> from nearby <a href="/wiki/Whiteman_Air_Force_Base" title="Whiteman Air Force Base">Whiteman Air Force Base</a>. Since the <a href="/wiki/1994_NFL_season" title="1994 NFL season">1994 NFL season</a>, the stadium has had a natural <a href="/wiki/Grass" class="mw-redirect" title="Grass">grass</a> playing surface.<sup id="cite_ref-This_Week,_February_2,_2008_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Week,_February_2,_2008-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> From 1972 to 1993, the stadium had an <a href="/wiki/Artificial_turf" title="Artificial turf">artificial</a> <a href="/wiki/AstroTurf" title="AstroTurf">AstroTurf</a> surface.<sup id="cite_ref-This_Week,_February_2,_2008_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-This_Week,_February_2,_2008-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the game against the Oakland Raiders on October 13, 2013, Arrowhead Stadium once again became the loudest stadium in the world when the fans set the Guinness Book of World Records record for loudest crowd in an outdoor stadium (137.5&#160;dB), breaking the record set by the Seattle Seahawks just four weeks prior. A few weeks after, Seattle re-gained the record by reaching a noise level of 137.6 decibels.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Chiefs fans have reclaimed the record once again on September 29, 2014, on <a href="/wiki/Monday_Night_Football" title="Monday Night Football">Monday Night Football</a> against the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Patriots" title="New England Patriots">New England Patriots</a>, the fans recorded a sound reading of 142.2 decibels.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Training_camp_and_practice_facility">Training camp and practice facility</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Training camp and practice facility">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mo-west-chiefs1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mo-west-chiefs1.jpg/220px-Mo-west-chiefs1.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="63" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mo-west-chiefs1.jpg/330px-Mo-west-chiefs1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Mo-west-chiefs1.jpg/440px-Mo-west-chiefs1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1794" data-file-height="510" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mo-west-chiefs1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Summer camp at <a href="/wiki/Spratt_Stadium" title="Spratt Stadium">Spratt Stadium</a> at Missouri Western</div></div></div>
<p>When the franchise was based in Dallas, the team conducted their inaugural <a href="/wiki/Training_camp_(National_Football_League)" title="Training camp (National Football League)">training camp</a> at the <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico_Military_Institute" title="New Mexico Military Institute">New Mexico Military Institute</a> in <a href="/wiki/Roswell,_New_Mexico" title="Roswell, New Mexico">Roswell, New Mexico</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-History_60s_12-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_60s-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> They moved camp to <a href="/wiki/Southern_Methodist_University" title="Southern Methodist University">Southern Methodist University</a>, owner Lamar Hunt's <a href="/wiki/Alma_mater" title="Alma mater">alma mater</a>, for 1961 and continued to practice there until 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-History_60s_12-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_60s-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> From 1966 to 1971, the Chiefs practiced in <a href="/wiki/Swope_Park" title="Swope Park">Swope Park</a> in Kansas City,<sup id="cite_ref-Peterson_176_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peterson_176-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> and from 1972 to 1991 held camp at <a href="/wiki/William_Jewell_College" title="William Jewell College">William Jewell College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Clay_County,_Missouri" title="Clay County, Missouri">Clay County, Missouri</a>–where Lamar Hunt had extensive business dealings including <a href="/wiki/Worlds_of_Fun" title="Worlds of Fun">Worlds of Fun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceans_of_Fun" title="Oceans of Fun">Oceans of Fun</a> and <a href="/wiki/SubTropolis" title="SubTropolis">SubTropolis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-History_90s_30-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_90s-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:011_KC_Chiefs_Practice_Fields.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/011_KC_Chiefs_Practice_Fields.jpg/220px-011_KC_Chiefs_Practice_Fields.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/011_KC_Chiefs_Practice_Fields.jpg/330px-011_KC_Chiefs_Practice_Fields.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/011_KC_Chiefs_Practice_Fields.jpg/440px-011_KC_Chiefs_Practice_Fields.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4200" data-file-height="2790" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:011_KC_Chiefs_Practice_Fields.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chiefs Practice Facility near Arrowhead Stadium</div></div></div>
<p>From 1992 to 2009 the Chiefs conducted summer training camp at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93River_Falls" title="University of Wisconsin–River Falls">University of Wisconsin–River Falls</a> in <a href="/wiki/River_Falls,_Wisconsin" title="River Falls, Wisconsin">River Falls, Wisconsin</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Au_Revoir,_River_Falls_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Au_Revoir,_River_Falls-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The Chiefs' 2007 training camp was documented in the <a href="/wiki/HBO" title="HBO">HBO</a>/<a href="/wiki/NFL_Films" title="NFL Films">NFL Films</a> <a href="/wiki/Documentary_film" title="Documentary film">documentary</a> <a href="/wiki/Reality_television" title="Reality television">reality television</a> series, <i><a href="/wiki/Hard_Knocks_(TV_series)" title="Hard Knocks (TV series)">Hard Knocks</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hard_Knocks_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hard_Knocks-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> Following the passage of a $25 million state tax credit proposal, the Chiefs moved their training camp to <a href="/wiki/Missouri_Western_State_University" title="Missouri Western State University">Missouri Western State University</a> in <a href="/wiki/St._Joseph,_Missouri" title="St. Joseph, Missouri">St. Joseph, Missouri</a>, in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs_announce_plans_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs_announce_plans-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> The bulk of the tax credits went for improvements to Arrowhead Stadium with $10 million applied to the move to Missouri Western.<sup id="cite_ref-St._Joseph_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-St._Joseph-95">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> A climate-controlled, 120-yard NFL regulation grass indoor field, and office space for the Chiefs was constructed at Missouri Western adjacent to the school's <a href="/wiki/Spratt_Stadium" title="Spratt Stadium">Spratt Stadium</a> before the 2010 season.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs_training_camp_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs_training_camp-96">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside of training camp and during the regular season, the Chiefs conduct practices at their own training facility nearby Arrowhead Stadium. The facility is located near the Raytown Road entrance to the Truman Sports Complex just east of <a href="/wiki/Interstate_435" title="Interstate 435">Interstate 435</a> and features three outdoor fields (two grass and one artificial turf) as well as an indoor facility with its own full-size field.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Rivalries">Rivalries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Rivalries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Chiefs%E2%80%93Raiders_rivalry" title="Chiefs–Raiders rivalry">Chiefs–Raiders rivalry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Broncos%E2%80%93Chiefs_rivalry" title="Broncos–Chiefs rivalry">Broncos–Chiefs rivalry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chargers%E2%80%93Chiefs_rivalry" title="Chargers–Chiefs rivalry">Chargers–Chiefs rivalry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Governor%27s_Cup_(Missouri)" title="Governor&#39;s Cup (Missouri)">Governor's Cup (Missouri)</a></div>
<p>The Chiefs share intense rivalries with their three AFC West opponents, namely the <a href="/wiki/Denver_Broncos" title="Denver Broncos">Denver Broncos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_Raiders" title="Las Vegas Raiders">Las Vegas Raiders</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Chargers" title="Los Angeles Chargers">Los Angeles Chargers</a>, with the Raiders rivalry considered one of the most bitter in the NFL.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> They also have had historical rivalries with the <a href="/wiki/Seattle_Seahawks" title="Seattle Seahawks">Seattle Seahawks</a>, who were a divisional rival until their reallocation to the <a href="/wiki/NFC_West" title="NFC West">NFC West</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> former AFL teams such as the <a href="/wiki/Buffalo_Bills" title="Buffalo Bills">Buffalo Bills</a>, and other AFC teams such as the <a href="/wiki/Baltimore_Ravens" title="Baltimore Ravens">Baltimore Ravens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indianapolis_Colts" title="Indianapolis Colts">Indianapolis Colts</a>. In terms of NFC teams, the Chiefs faced off once in the Super Bowl against the <a href="/wiki/Green_Bay_Packers" title="Green Bay Packers">Green Bay Packers</a>, and formerly shared a <a href="/wiki/Governor%27s_Cup_(Missouri)" title="Governor&#39;s Cup (Missouri)">cross-state rivalry</a> with teams located across the state of Missouri in <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a>, namely the <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Cardinals_(football)" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis Cardinals (football)">Cardinals</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Louis_Rams" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Louis Rams">Rams</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mascots_and_cheerleaders">Mascots and cheerleaders</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Mascots and cheerleaders">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:KC_Wolf.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/KC_Wolf.JPG/220px-KC_Wolf.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="287" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/KC_Wolf.JPG/330px-KC_Wolf.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/ac/KC_Wolf.JPG 2x" data-file-width="367" data-file-height="479" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:KC_Wolf.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/K._C._Wolf" title="K. C. Wolf">K. C. Wolf</a>, the Chiefs' mascot since 1989</div></div></div>
<p>The Chiefs' first mascot was <a href="/wiki/Warpaint_(mascot)" title="Warpaint (mascot)">Warpaint</a>, a nickname given to several different breeds of <a href="/wiki/Pinto_horse" title="Pinto horse">pinto horse</a>. Warpaint served as the team's mascot from 1963 to 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-Althaus_35_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Althaus_35-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mascots,_cheerleaders_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mascots,_cheerleaders-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McKenzie_141_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_141-101">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The first Warpaint (born in 1955, died in 1992) was ridden bareback by rider Bob Johnson who wore a full <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Native American</a> <a href="/wiki/Headdress" class="mw-redirect" title="Headdress">headdress</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Althaus_35_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Althaus_35-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mascots,_cheerleaders_100-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mascots,_cheerleaders-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Warpaint circled the field at the beginning of each Chiefs home game and performed victory laps following each Chiefs touchdown.<sup id="cite_ref-Althaus_35_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Althaus_35-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mascots,_cheerleaders_100-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mascots,_cheerleaders-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> On September 20, 2009, a new Warpaint horse was unveiled at the Chiefs' home opener.<sup id="cite_ref-Warpaint_returns_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warpaint_returns-102">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Warpaint is now ridden by a cheerleader, Susie.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the mid-1980s, the Chiefs featured a short-lived unnamed "Indian man" mascot which was later scrapped in 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-mascots,_cheerleaders_100-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mascots,_cheerleaders-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Since 1989 the cartoon-like <a href="/wiki/K._C._Wolf" title="K. C. Wolf">K. C. Wolf</a>, portrayed by Dan Meers in a <a href="/wiki/Wolf" title="Wolf">wolf</a> costume, has served as the team's mascot.<sup id="cite_ref-Althaus_35_11-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Althaus_35-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-K._C._Wolf_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-K._C._Wolf-104">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The mascot was named after the Chiefs' "Wolfpack", a group of rabid fans from the team's days at Municipal Stadium.<sup id="cite_ref-mascots,_cheerleaders_100-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mascots,_cheerleaders-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> K. C. Wolf is one of the most popular NFL mascots and was the league's first mascot inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Mascot_Hall_of_Fame" title="Mascot Hall of Fame">Mascot Hall of Fame</a> in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-Mascot_Hall_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mascot_Hall-105">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chiefs have employed a <a href="/wiki/Kansas_City_Chiefs_Cheerleaders" title="Kansas City Chiefs Cheerleaders">cheerleading squad</a> since the team's inception in 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-McKenzie_140_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_140-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> In the team's early days, the all-female squad was referred to as the Chiefettes.<sup id="cite_ref-History_cheer_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_cheer-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> In addition to the Cheerleaders, in the early 1970s, there was also a dance/drill team that performed for pre-game and halftime. From 1986 to 1992, the cheerleader squad featured a mix of men and women.<sup id="cite_ref-McKenzie_140_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_140-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Since 1993, the all-female squad has been known as the Chiefs Cheerleaders.<sup id="cite_ref-mascots,_cheerleaders_100-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mascots,_cheerleaders-100">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McKenzie_140_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_140-106">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-History_cheer_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_cheer-107">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pro_Football_Hall_of_Fame_enshrinees">Pro Football Hall of Fame enshrinees</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Pro Football Hall of Fame enshrinees">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Twenty-three members of the Pro Football Hall of Fame spent at least some portion of their career with the Chiefs. Thirteen spent the majority of the career with the Chiefs. Ten of the Chiefs in the Hall of Fame were involved with the Chiefs during their <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_IV" title="Super Bowl IV">Super Bowl Championship</a> season of <a href="/wiki/1969_Kansas_City_Chiefs_season" title="1969 Kansas City Chiefs season">1969</a>. The Chiefs have 3 contributors, 2 coaches, and 18 players in the Hall of Fame. In addition, Hall of Fame safety <a href="/wiki/Ronnie_Lott" title="Ronnie Lott">Ronnie Lott</a> had a contract with the Chiefs during the 1995 season, but only played for the Chiefs in the preseason and spent the regular season on injured reserve and is not recognized by the Hall of Fame as having played for the Chiefs.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chiefs_Hall_of_Fame">Chiefs Hall of Fame</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chiefs Hall of Fame">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jan_Stenerud.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Jan_Stenerud.JPG/220px-Jan_Stenerud.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Jan_Stenerud.JPG/330px-Jan_Stenerud.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Jan_Stenerud.JPG/440px-Jan_Stenerud.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2576" data-file-height="1932" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jan_Stenerud.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Jan_Stenerud" title="Jan Stenerud">Jan Stenerud</a>'s name was honored at Arrowhead Stadium's ring of honor<div style="clear:both;"></div></div></div></div>
<p>The Chiefs are one of 16 organizations that honor their players, coaches and contributors with a team Hall of Fame or Ring of Honor.<sup id="cite_ref-Who&#39;s_next_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Who&#39;s_next-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Established in 1970, the Chiefs Hall of Fame has inducted a new member in an annual ceremony with the exception of the 1983 season.<sup id="cite_ref-Who&#39;s_next_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Who&#39;s_next-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs_Hall_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs_Hall-109">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Several of the names were featured at Arrowhead Stadium in the stadium's architecture prior to renovations in 2009. The requirements for induction are that a player, coach, or contributor must have been with the Chiefs for four seasons and been out of the NFL for four seasons at the time of induction.<sup id="cite_ref-Who&#39;s_next_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Who&#39;s_next-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> There are some exceptions, such as <a href="/wiki/Joe_Delaney" title="Joe Delaney">Joe Delaney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Derrick_Thomas" title="Derrick Thomas">Derrick Thomas</a>, Delaney was with the team for only two seasons before his death, Thomas was inducted 1 year after his death in January 2000 (2 years after his final season). The Chiefs have the second-most enshrinees of any NFL team in their team Hall of Fame behind the <a href="/wiki/Green_Bay_Packers" title="Green Bay Packers">Green Bay Packers</a>, who have enshrined over 100 players and team contributors over the years in the <a href="/wiki/Green_Bay_Packers_Hall_of_Fame" title="Green Bay Packers Hall of Fame">Green Bay Packers Hall of Fame</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Who&#39;s_next_108-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Who&#39;s_next-108">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Nineteen players and the coach of the <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_IV" title="Super Bowl IV">Super Bowl IV</a> championship team have been inducted into the ring of honor.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Head_coaches">Head coaches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Head coaches">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Andy_Reid_at_Eagles_training_camp_2010-08-03.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Andy_Reid_at_Eagles_training_camp_2010-08-03.jpg/200px-Andy_Reid_at_Eagles_training_camp_2010-08-03.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Andy_Reid_at_Eagles_training_camp_2010-08-03.jpg/300px-Andy_Reid_at_Eagles_training_camp_2010-08-03.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Andy_Reid_at_Eagles_training_camp_2010-08-03.jpg/400px-Andy_Reid_at_Eagles_training_camp_2010-08-03.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1224" data-file-height="1523" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Andy_Reid_at_Eagles_training_camp_2010-08-03.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Andy_Reid" title="Andy Reid">Andy Reid</a> (pictured in 2010), began his first season as the Chiefs' head coach in 2013</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Kansas_City_Chiefs_head_coaches" title="List of Kansas City Chiefs head coaches">List of Kansas City Chiefs head coaches</a></div>
<p>Thirteen head coaches have served the Texans/Chiefs franchise since their first season in 1960. <a href="/wiki/Hank_Stram" title="Hank Stram">Hank Stram</a>, the team's first head coach, led the Chiefs to three AFL championship victories and two appearances in the <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl" title="Super Bowl">Super Bowl</a>. Stram was the team's longest-tenured head coach, holding the position from 1960 to 1974.<sup id="cite_ref-History_70s_22-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_70s-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marty_Schottenheimer" title="Marty Schottenheimer">Marty Schottenheimer</a> was hired in 1989 and led Kansas City to seven playoff appearances in his 10 seasons as head coach.<sup id="cite_ref-History_80s_25-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_80s-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-History_90s_30-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_90s-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> Schottenheimer had the best <a href="/wiki/Winning_percentage" title="Winning percentage">winning percentage</a> (.634) of all Chiefs coaches.<sup id="cite_ref-Schottenheimer_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schottenheimer-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gunther_Cunningham" title="Gunther Cunningham">Gunther Cunningham</a> was on the Chiefs' coaching staff in various positions from 1995 to 2008, serving as the team's head coach in between stints as the team's <a href="/wiki/Defensive_coordinator" title="Defensive coordinator">defensive coordinator</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs_name_Gunther_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs_name_Gunther-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-History_2000s_34-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_2000s-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dick_Vermeil" title="Dick Vermeil">Dick Vermeil</a> coached the team to a franchise-best 9–0 start in the <a href="/wiki/2003_Kansas_City_Chiefs_season" title="2003 Kansas City Chiefs season">2003 season</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Curtain_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Curtain-110">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Of the ten Chiefs coaches, <a href="/wiki/Hank_Stram" title="Hank Stram">Hank Stram</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marv_Levy" title="Marv Levy">Marv Levy</a> have been elected into the <a href="/wiki/Pro_Football_Hall_of_Fame" title="Pro Football Hall of Fame">Pro Football Hall of Fame</a>, Levy, however, is more well known for his time with the Buffalo Bills.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Herman_Edwards" class="mw-redirect" title="Herman Edwards">Herman Edwards</a> served as the team's head coach from 2006 to 2008, compiling a 15–33 record and a franchise worst 6–26 record over a two-year span.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs_fire_Edwards_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs_fire_Edwards-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Edwards_relieved_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards_relieved-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Edwards_named_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards_named-112">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-permission_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-permission-113">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Todd_Haley" title="Todd Haley">Todd Haley</a> compiled a 19–26 record with the team from 2009–2011, including an AFC West division title in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Haley_named_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haley_named-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Haley was fired with three games left in the 2011 season. <a href="/wiki/Romeo_Crennel" title="Romeo Crennel">Romeo Crennel</a> was named interim coach, and was promoted to full-time coach in January 2012. Crennel was fired on Monday, December 31, 2012, after finishing the 2012 season with a 2–14 record. On January 5, 2013, the Chiefs hired <a href="/wiki/Andy_Reid" title="Andy Reid">Andy Reid</a> to be their next head coach. In Reid's tenure, the Chiefs have been consistent contenders, making the playoffs in 6 of his 7 seasons as head coach, winning the division four times.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ownership_and_administration">Ownership and administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Ownership and administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clark_Hunt.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Clark_Hunt.JPG/200px-Clark_Hunt.JPG" decoding="async" width="200" height="297" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Clark_Hunt.JPG/300px-Clark_Hunt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Clark_Hunt.JPG/400px-Clark_Hunt.JPG 2x" data-file-width="468" data-file-height="694" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clark_Hunt.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chairman of the Board and co-owner <a href="/wiki/Clark_Hunt" title="Clark Hunt">Clark Hunt</a></div></div></div>
<p>The franchise was founded in 1959 by Lamar Hunt after a failed attempt by Hunt to purchase an NFL franchise and relocate them to Texas.<sup id="cite_ref-Media_4_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_4-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>  Hunt remained the team's owner until his death in 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-Media_4_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_4-114">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> The Hunt family kept ownership of the team following Lamar's death and <a href="/wiki/Clark_Hunt" title="Clark Hunt">Clark Hunt</a>, Lamar's son, represents the family's interests.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> While Hunt's official title is CEO and <a href="/wiki/Chairman" class="mw-redirect" title="Chairman">Chairman of the Board</a>, he represents the team at all owner meetings.<sup id="cite_ref-Clark_Hunt_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clark_Hunt-115">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media_6_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_6-116">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> In 2010, Hunt assumed role as <a href="/wiki/Chief_executive_officer" title="Chief executive officer">CEO</a> alongside his role as Chairman of the Board.<sup id="cite_ref-Thum_steps_down_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thum_steps_down-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i>, the team is valued at $2.4 billion and ranks 24th among NFL teams in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-Forbes_7-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Forbes-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Owner Lamar Hunt served as the team's president from 1960 to 1976. Because of Lamar Hunt's contributions to the NFL, the AFC Championship trophy is named after him.<sup id="cite_ref-Media_411_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_411-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> He promoted general manager <a href="/wiki/Jack_Steadman" title="Jack Steadman">Jack Steadman</a> to become the team's president in 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-Media_411_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_411-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Steadman held the job until <a href="/wiki/Carl_Peterson" title="Carl Peterson">Carl Peterson</a> was hired by Hunt in 1988 to replace him.<sup id="cite_ref-Media_411_118-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_411-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Peterson resigned the title as team president in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs&#39;_Carl_Peterson_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs&#39;_Carl_Peterson-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Denny_Thum" title="Denny Thum">Denny Thum</a> became the team's interim president following Peterson's departure and was officially given the full position in May 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs&#39;_Carl_Peterson_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs&#39;_Carl_Peterson-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thum_named_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thum_named-120">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> Thum resigned from his position on September 14, 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-Thum_steps_down_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thum_steps_down-117">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Don_Rossi" title="Don Rossi">Don Rossi</a> served as the team's general manager for half of the 1960 season, resigning in November 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-History_60s_12-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_60s-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Jack Steadman assumed duties from Rossi and served in the position until 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-History_60s_12-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_60s-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-History_70s_22-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_70s-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media_411_118-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_411-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Steadman was promoted to team president in 1976 and despite being relieved of those duties in 1988,<sup id="cite_ref-Media_411_118-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_411-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> he remained with the franchise until 2006 in various positions.<sup id="cite_ref-History_70s_22-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_70s-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-History_80s_25-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_80s-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jim_Schaaf" title="Jim Schaaf">Jim Schaaf</a> took over for Steadman as general manager until being fired in December 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-History_80s_25-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_80s-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Carl Peterson was hired in 1988 to serve as the team's general manager, chief executive officer and team president.<sup id="cite_ref-History_80s_25-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History_80s-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Media_411_118-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_411-118">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> Peterson remained in the position for 19 years until he announced his resignation from the team in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs&#39;_Carl_Peterson_119-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs&#39;_Carl_Peterson-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-press_conference_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-press_conference-121">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Denny Thum served as interim general manager<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs&#39;_Carl_Peterson_119-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs&#39;_Carl_Peterson-119">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup> until January 13, 2009, when the Chiefs named <a href="/wiki/New_England_Patriots" title="New England Patriots">New England Patriots</a> executive <a href="/wiki/Scott_Pioli" title="Scott Pioli">Scott Pioli</a> the team's new general manager.<sup id="cite_ref-Pioli_to_join_60-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pioli_to_join-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pioli_named_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pioli_named-122">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
Pioli was released in early January after the hiring of Andy Reid, and was replaced by John Dorsey. Pioli's record as the Chief's general manager was 23–41.
</p><p>On June 22, 2017, it was announced that the Chiefs had fired Dorsey.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> They hired <a href="/wiki/Brett_Veach" title="Brett Veach">Brett Veach</a> as the new general manager on July 10, 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Staff">Staff</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Staff">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Media">Media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_and_television">Radio and television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Radio and television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="wikitable" style="font-size:90%; margin: 0.5em 1em 0.5em 0; float: right; clear: right;">
<caption style="background: #E31837; color: #FFFFFF; -moz-box-shadow: inset 2px 2px 0 #FFB612, inset -2px -2px 0 #FFB612; -webkit-box-shadow: inset 2px 2px 0 #FFB612, inset -2px -2px 0 #FFB612; box-shadow: inset 2px 2px 0 #FFB612, inset -2px -2px 0 #FFB612;;">Kansas City Chiefs radio play-by-play announcers<sup id="cite_ref-Media_51_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_51-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<td>1960–1962
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Charlie_Jones_(sportscaster)" title="Charlie Jones (sportscaster)">Charlie Jones</a>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>1963
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Merle_Harmon" title="Merle Harmon">Merle Harmon</a>
</td></tr>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>
<tr>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>1994–
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Mitch_Holthus" title="Mitch Holthus">Mitch Holthus</a>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>From 1989 until the team's Super Bowl LIV victory, <a href="/wiki/Cumulus_Media" title="Cumulus Media">Cumulus Media</a>'s <a href="/wiki/KCFX" title="KCFX">KCFX</a>, a.k.a. "101 The Fox", broadcast all Chiefs games on <a href="/wiki/FM_broadcasting" title="FM broadcasting">FM radio</a> under the moniker of The Chiefs Fox Football Radio Network. Since 1994, <a href="/wiki/Mitch_Holthus" title="Mitch Holthus">Mitch Holthus</a> has served as <a href="/wiki/Play-by-play" class="mw-redirect" title="Play-by-play">play-by-play</a> announcer and former Chiefs quarterback Len Dawson serves as <a href="/wiki/Color_commentator" title="Color commentator">color commentator</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Media_51_125-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_51-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Former Chiefs <a href="/wiki/Longsnapper" class="mw-redirect" title="Longsnapper">longsnapper</a> <a href="/wiki/Kendall_Gammon" title="Kendall Gammon">Kendall Gammon</a> serves as the field reporter.<sup id="cite_ref-Media_51_125-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_51-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Former Chiefs broadcaster <a href="/wiki/Bob_Gretz" title="Bob Gretz">Bob Gretz</a> also contributes to the broadcasts. Starting in the <a href="/wiki/2016_Kansas_City_Chiefs_season" title="2016 Kansas City Chiefs season">2016 season</a>, Dawson will only serve as color commentator during home games, and Gammon will be color commentator during road games, with Dani Welniak assuming Gammon's sideline reporting role for away games.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> The Chiefs and KCFX held the distinction of being the longest FM radio broadcast partnering tenure in the NFL.<sup id="cite_ref-Media_51_125-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_51-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101_The_Fox_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101_The_Fox-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> The Chiefs Radio Network extends throughout the six-state region of Missouri, <a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iowa" title="Iowa">Iowa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nebraska" title="Nebraska">Nebraska</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, with 61 affiliate stations.<sup id="cite_ref-Media_51_125-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Media_51-125">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101_The_Fox_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101_The_Fox-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>With the start of the 2020 season, <a href="/wiki/Entercom" title="Entercom">Entercom</a>'s <a href="/wiki/WDAF-FM" title="WDAF-FM">WDAF-FM</a> (106.5) will become the flagship of the Chiefs network, with sister station <a href="/wiki/KCSP_(AM)" title="KCSP (AM)">KCSP</a> (610) carrying surrounding analysis and team interview programming. The network's personnel, outside those exclusively contracted to Cumulus, are expected to be retained with the new Entercom contract.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/KCTV" title="KCTV">KCTV Channel 5</a> (<a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>) broadcasts most Chiefs regular season games, with exceptions as following. KCTV also broadcasts all Chiefs <a href="/wiki/National_Football_League_exhibition_season" class="mw-redirect" title="National Football League exhibition season">pre-season</a> games. <a href="/wiki/WDAF-TV" title="WDAF-TV">WDAF Channel 4</a> (<a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">Fox</a>; that station is unrelated to WDAF-FM) broadcasts games in which the Chiefs host an NFC opponent, and carried the team's Super Bowl LIV victory. <a href="/wiki/KSHB-TV" title="KSHB-TV">KSHB Channel 41</a> (<a href="/wiki/NBC" title="NBC">NBC</a>) broadcasts all games in which the Chiefs play on <i><a href="/wiki/NBC_Sunday_Night_Football" title="NBC Sunday Night Football">NBC Sunday Night Football</a></i> or NBC's NFL playoffs coverage. <a href="/wiki/KMBC-TV" title="KMBC-TV">KMBC Channel 9</a> (<a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a>) has aired <i><a href="/wiki/Monday_Night_Football" title="Monday Night Football">Monday Night Football</a></i> games locally since 1970.
</p><p>Prior to the 1994 season, WDAF was the primary station for the Chiefs as an NBC affiliate (they aired on KMBC when ABC had the AFL package through 1964), since NBC had the AFC package. The inter-conference home games aired on KCTV starting in 1973 (when the NFL allowed local telecasts of home games). After week one of the 1994 season, WDAF switched to Fox (which got the NFC package), and has aired the Chiefs' inter-conference home games since. The bulk of the team's games moved to KSHB through the end of the 1997 season. Since that time, they have aired on KCTV, save for the 2015 Week 17 game vs. the <a href="/wiki/Oakland_Raiders" class="mw-redirect" title="Oakland Raiders">Oakland Raiders</a>, which aired on WDAF when the NFL cross-flexed the game from CBS to FOX.
</p><p>As of the 2015 preseason, the Chiefs preseason broadcasters were <a href="/wiki/Paul_Burmeister" title="Paul Burmeister">Paul Burmeister</a> who serves as the play-by-play announcer, former Chiefs quarterback <a href="/wiki/Trent_Green" title="Trent Green">Trent Green</a> serves as the color commentator, and KCChiefs.com insider B.J. Kissel is the sideline reporter.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio_affiliates">Radio affiliates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Radio affiliates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kansas_City_Chiefs_radio_affiliates.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kansas_City_Chiefs_radio_affiliates.png/220px-Kansas_City_Chiefs_radio_affiliates.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kansas_City_Chiefs_radio_affiliates.png/330px-Kansas_City_Chiefs_radio_affiliates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Kansas_City_Chiefs_radio_affiliates.png/440px-Kansas_City_Chiefs_radio_affiliates.png 2x" data-file-width="3552" data-file-height="2880" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kansas_City_Chiefs_radio_affiliates.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of radio affiliates.</div></div></div>
<p>Chiefs games are broadcast in Missouri and Kansas as well as parts of Iowa, Oklahoma, Nebraska, Arkansas, and South Dakota.<sup id="cite_ref-RadioAffiliates_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RadioAffiliates-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Stations in major cities are listed below.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fan_base">Fan base</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Fan base">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:061123Broncos-Chiefs01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/061123Broncos-Chiefs01.jpg/220px-061123Broncos-Chiefs01.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/061123Broncos-Chiefs01.jpg/330px-061123Broncos-Chiefs01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/061123Broncos-Chiefs01.jpg/440px-061123Broncos-Chiefs01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2576" data-file-height="1932" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:061123Broncos-Chiefs01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Arrowhead Stadium</div></div></div>
<p>The Chiefs boast one of the most loyal fan bases in the NFL.<sup id="cite_ref-NFL&#39;s_best_fans_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NFL&#39;s_best_fans-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Insider_grading_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Insider_grading-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Kansas City is the sixth-smallest <a href="/wiki/Media_market" title="Media market">media market</a> with an NFL team, but they have had the second-highest attendance average over the last decade.<sup id="cite_ref-NFL&#39;s_Top_10_Fan_Bases_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NFL&#39;s_Top_10_Fan_Bases-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Studies by <a href="/wiki/American_City_Business_Journals" title="American City Business Journals">Bizjournals</a> in 2006 gave the Chiefs high marks for consistently drawing capacity crowds in both good seasons and bad.<sup id="cite_ref-Cleveland_is_No._1_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cleveland_is_No._1-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The Chiefs averaged 77,300 fans per game from 1996 to 2006, second in the NFL behind the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Redskins" title="Washington Redskins">Washington Redskins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Cleveland_is_No._1_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cleveland_is_No._1-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The franchise has an official fan club called <b>Chiefs Kingdom</b> which gives members opportunities to ticket priority benefits and VIP treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-Chiefs_Nation_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiefs_Nation-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-birth_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-birth-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of "<a href="/wiki/The_Star-Spangled_Banner" title="The Star-Spangled Banner">The Star-Spangled Banner</a>" before home games, many Chiefs fans intentionally yell out "CHIEFS!" rather than singing "brave" as the final word.<sup id="cite_ref-McKenzie_132_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_132-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> In 1996, general manager Carl Peterson said "We all look forward, not only at Arrowhead, but on the road, too, to when we get to that stanza of the National Anthem... Our players love it."<sup id="cite_ref-McKenzie_132_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_132-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" class="mw-redirect" title="September 11, 2001 attacks">September 11, 2001 attacks</a>, Chiefs fans refrained from doing so in honor of those who lost their lives in the tragedy and continued to do so for the remainder of the 2001 season.<sup id="cite_ref-Giants_beat_Chiefs_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Giants_beat_Chiefs-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> At the Chiefs' September 23, 2001, home game against the New York Giants, fans gave the opposing Giants a standing ovation.<sup id="cite_ref-Peter_King_Notes_–_The_NFL_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peter_King_Notes_–_The_NFL-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>After every Chiefs touchdown at home games, fans chant while pointing in the direction of the visiting team and fans, "We're gonna beat the hell outta you...you...you, you, you, you!" over the song "<a href="/wiki/Rock_and_Roll_(Gary_Glitter_song)" title="Rock and Roll (Gary Glitter song)">Rock and Roll Part 2</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-Celebration_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Celebration-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The chant starts after the third "hey!" in the song.<sup id="cite_ref-Celebration_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Celebration-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The original version of the song by <a href="/wiki/Gary_Glitter" title="Gary Glitter">Gary Glitter</a> was previously used until the NFL banned his music from its facilities in 2006 following the British rocker's conviction on sexual abuse charges in Vietnam.<sup id="cite_ref-Celebration_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Celebration-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> A cover version of the song played by <a href="/wiki/Tube_Tops_2000" title="Tube Tops 2000">Tube Tops 2000</a><sup id="cite_ref-Celebration_136-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Celebration-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> was used from 2006 until the 2015 season.<sup id="cite_ref-New_Celebration_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-New_Celebration-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chiefs' fan base has expanded across the world like many other NFL teams. However, there is a <a href="/wiki/Twitter" title="Twitter">Twitter</a> account dedicated to Chiefs fans in the UK and has been recognized by the Kansas City Chiefs and is their official UK fan page. They have many dedicated fans writing articles and interviewing players of the team such as <a href="/wiki/Tamba_Hali" title="Tamba Hali">Tamba Hali</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Arrowhead Stadium is also recognized by <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness World Records</a> as having the loudest outdoor stadium in the world. This was achieved on September 29, 2014 in a <a href="/wiki/Monday_Night_Football" title="Monday Night Football">Monday Night Football</a> game against the <a href="/wiki/New_England_Patriots" title="New England Patriots">New England Patriots</a> when the crowd achieved a roar of 142.2 <a href="/wiki/Decibels" class="mw-redirect" title="Decibels">decibels</a><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> which is comparable to standing 100 feet (30&#160;m) from a jet engine, which even with short term exposure, can cause permanent damage.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tomahawk_chop">Tomahawk chop</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Tomahawk chop">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Native_American_mascot_controversy" title="Native American mascot controversy">Native American mascot controversy</a></div>
<p>Chiefs fans also carry on a tradition that began at Florida State University in the mid 1980s by using the Seminole WarChant as a rallying cry during key moments in their football games.<sup id="cite_ref-McKenzie_133_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_133-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Prior to each home game, a former Chiefs player<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> or famous Chiefs fan (such as NASCAR driver <a href="/wiki/Clint_Bowyer" title="Clint Bowyer">Clint Bowyer</a><sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> or rapper <a href="/wiki/Tech_N9ne" title="Tech N9ne">Tech N9ne</a><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>), called the honorary drum leader, bangs on a drum with a large drum stick to start the <a href="/wiki/Tomahawk_chop" title="Tomahawk chop">Tomahawk chop</a>.
</p><p>The Chiefs' and their fans use of American Indian imagery and stereotypes has been the source of controversy, as some decry usage of symbols like the "war drum," songs and spectacles like the "Tomahawk Chop," and stereotypical dress of fans in faux war paint and headdresses.  In 2016, Native American groups asked the Kansas City Chiefs to stop doing the tomahawk chop.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> In the same year a similar request was made of Exeter Chiefs.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>  The Editorial Board of the Kansas City Star newspaper called for the cessation of the "Tomahawk Chop" in late 2019, noting opposition from Native Americans and Tribes, and stating that the practice stereotypes and dehumanizes Native Americans.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Chiefs have escaped the more intense criticism of other teams using Native American names and logos, however attention increased in 2020 in advance of their appearance in <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_LIV" title="Super Bowl LIV">Super Bowl LIV</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> While there has been efforts to address other issues, such as fans wearing warpaint and headdresses, the "chop" and the accompanying chant is defended, including by some local Native Americans. However, in a national survey, half of Native Americans said the "tomahawk chop" bothered or offended them, rising to 65% among those more engaged in Native traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tony_DiPardo">Tony DiPardo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Kansas_City_Chiefs&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Tony DiPardo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From various periods between 1963 and the 2008 season, <a href="/wiki/Trumpet" title="Trumpet">trumpeter</a> Tony DiPardo and The T.D. Pack Band played live music at every Chiefs home game.<sup id="cite_ref-Althaus_86_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Althaus_86-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McKenzie_137_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_137-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> The band was known as The Zing Band when the team was located at Municipal Stadium. DiPardo was honored by head coach Hank Stram in 1969 with a <a href="/wiki/Super_Bowl_ring" title="Super Bowl ring">Super Bowl ring</a> for the team's victory in Super Bowl IV.<sup id="cite_ref-Althaus_86_151-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Althaus_86-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> When his health was declining, DiPardo took a leave of absence from the band from 1983 to 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-McKenzie_137_152-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_137-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> DiPardo's daughter took over as bandleader in 1989, by which time DiPardo returned to the band by popular demand.<sup id="cite_ref-McKenzie_137_152-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McKenzie_137-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Althaus_100_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Althaus_100-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> For the 2009 season, due to renovations at Arrowhead Stadium, the band did not return to perform at the stadium.
</p><p>DiPardo died on January 27, 2011, at age 98. He had been hospitalized since December 2010 after suffering a brain aneurysm.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
